<script>

    function resetBanner(doi) {
        $.get('/action/getAccount?doi=' + doi, function(data) {
            if (data) {
                $(".csas-institution-banner p .accountName").html(data);
                $(".csas-institution-banner").fadeIn();
            }
        });
    }

    var flag = 0;
    $(document).ready(function() {
        $(".banner-close").click(function() {
            $.get("/action/dismissBanner", function(data) {
                $(".csas-institution-banner").fadeOut();
            });
        });

        $(".articleContent .tabs li[role='tab'].showFullText").click(function() {
           flag = 1;
        });

        $(document).on("reloadTabsContent", function() {
            var isSelected = $(".articleContent .tabs li[role='tab'].showFullText").hasClass("active");
            if (isSelected) {
                resetBanner($(".csas-institution-banner").data("doi"));
                console.log("fadeIn");
            } else {
                $(".csas-institution-banner").fadeOut();
                console.log("fadeOut");
            }
            flag = 0;
        });

        $(document).on("click", ".articleContent .tabs li[role='tab']", function() {

        });
    });
</script></div>

NA, no addition.</p><p>(D and E) Demonstration that DMB decreases plasma TMAO levels in male (D) and female (E) C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice placed chronically on either a 1.0% choline-supplemented or a 1.0% carnitine-supplemented diet in the presence versus absence of DMB (1%, v/v in drinking water).</p><p>(F) Three groups of male C57BL/6J <em>Apo</em>e<sup>-/-</sup> mice (8 weeks old) were placed on a chemically defined diet equivalent to chow supplemented with choline (1.0% total choline). Provision of DMB in the drinking water of atherosclerosis-prone <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice inhibits choline-diet-dependent enhancement in TMAO, endogenous macrophage foam cell formation, and atherosclerosis development.</p></div></div><div class="hiddenLargeImg" id="image-S0092867415015743figs1"><div class="links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr6" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768670/gr6.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">Prev</a>----

At the indicated time points, TMA lyase activity was measured as d9-TMA production using stable isotope dilution LC/MS/MS.</p><p>(B) Effect of the indicated choline analogs on choline TMA lyase activity of <em>P. mirabilis</em> lysate. Protein lysate (3 mg) was incubated with d9-choline (100 M) in 2 ml PBS with the indicated concentrations of choline analogs (P-choline, betaine, TMSi-ETOH, and DMB) at 37C for 23 hr in gas tight reaction vials and choline TMA lyase activity measured as the production of d9-TMA, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>.</p><p>(C) <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells were transformed with pUC57 plasmid (negative control) or pUC57 expression vectors constitutively expressing <em>D. alaskensis cutC</em> alone, <em>cutD</em> alone, <em>cutC-</em> intergenic region-<em>cutD</em>, or the indicated catalytically inactive <em>cutC</em> point mutants, as described in <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. Bacterial cells at stationary phase (OD<sub>600 nm</sub> = 1.5), were pelleted, re-suspended in 6x original culture volume of cold argon-sparged PBS containing d9-choline (100 M) substrate  DMB (2 mM) and incubated in gas-tight reaction vials under argon blanket at 37C for 12 hr. Choline TMA lyase activity was quantified by the production of d9-TMA by stable isotope dilution LC/MS/MS.</p><p>Data represent mean  SE from three independent replicates (A-C). CutC was N-terminally tagged with an 8x-His-tag, and CutD was C-terminally tagged with Strep-Tag II, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. As an additional negative control, cells were also transformed with the empty vector. Shown is the SDS-PAGE with Coomassie blue staining of bacterial lysates from the indicated transformed cells. Identity of bands as CutC and CutD was confirmed by excising of each band, and performing LC/MS/MS analysis on in-gel tryptic digests (data not shown).</p><p>(BE) <em>P. mirabilis cutC/D-</em>transformed, or empty-vector transformed <em>E. coli</em> Stellar cells were grown up to stationary phase (OD<sub>600</sub> = 1.5), harvested by centrifugation and re-suspended in 1x PBS with a volume of 6:1 (original culture volume), and then incubated with 100 M of the indicated d9- labeled substrates  DMB (2 mM) at 37C for 24 hr in gas-tight reaction vials, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. As an additional (negative) control, isotope-labeled substrates in PBS without addition of cells were incubated in sealed reaction vials. d9-TMA produced was measured by LC/MS/MS.</p><p>(F) SDS-PAGE analysis of purified CutC after Ni-NTA IMAC affinity chromatography, and purified CutD by Strep-Tactin affinity chromatography.</p><p>(G and H) Purified CutC (30 g) and CutD (30 g) were incubated together in PBS (1 ml) supplemented with Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (2 mM) and S-adenosyl-L-methionine (1 mM) in the presence of isotope-labeled substrate (100 M of either d9-choline (G) or d9-GPC (H)) and the indicated concentrations of DMB, in gas-tight reaction vials under anaerobic conditions at 37C for 12 hr. d9-TMA produced was then quantified by LC/MS/MS. Reactions without CutC or CutD were used as blank controls for each individual d9-TMA containing compound (d9-choline or d9-GPC) and showed negligible (~0.25 nM) d9-TMA production.</p><p>Data (B-E,G,H) represent mean  SE from 3 independent replicates.</p></div></div><div class="hiddenLargeImg" id="image-S0092867415015743figs3"><div class="links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">Prev</a>----

                    ----<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768663/figs5.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">Next</a></div><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" alt="Large image of Figure S4." title="Large image of Figure S4."></img><div class="caption"><p class="caption-title"><span class="label">Figure S4</span></p><p>DMB Inhibits Atherosclerosis in Female <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> Mice on a High-Choline Diet, Affects Flavin Monooxygenase Activity, and Is Not Detected in Urine if Orally Administered, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig3">Figures 3</span></span> and <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">4</span></span></p><p>(A) C57BL/6J Apoe<sup>-/-</sup> mice at time of weaning (4 weeks) were placed on chemically defined chow diet (0.07% total choline) or chow diet supplemented with choline (1.0% total choline), with or without 1% DMB added to the drinking water. At 20 weeks of age, mice were sacrificed. Hearts/aorta from mice were fixed and processed for aortic lesion area quantification, as described in <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. Data shown represent mean  SE from the indicated number of female mice per treatment group (data for male mice is shown in main manuscript).</p><p>(B) Liver tissue homogenate from male mice (1 mg/ml protein) was incubated with d9-TMA (100 M) in PBS supplemented with and without NADPH (100 M) at 37C for 8 hr. Reactions were stopped by adding 0.1 N formic acid, and d9-TMAO formed quantified by LC/MS/MS using d4-choline as internal standard. Total hepatic FMO activity was calculated as the NADPH-dependent d9-TMAO formed. Data represent mean  SE from the indicated number of mice.</p><p>(C) Identification of DMB in urine by GC/MS. GC chromatograms of blank (violet), DMB standard spiked into urine (green), urine recovered 30 min after gastric gavage of mice with 1.9 l (15 mol) DMB in 200 l saline (red), urine recovered 30 min after intraperitoneal injection (ip) of mice with 1.9 l (15 mol) DMB in 200 l saline (14.2 M DMB detected) (blue). The red dotted line indicates the retention time of DMB (3,3-dimethyl-1-butanol). p values shown were calculated by ANOVA.</p></div></div><div class="hiddenLargeImg" id="image-S0092867415015743figs5"><div class="links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">Prev</a>----

The prepared compound, P-choline, was dissolved in water at a concentration of 50 M, and was injected onto an AB SCIEX TripleTOF 5600 LC-MS/MS System at a flow rate of 10 l/min with an electrospray interface operating in positive TOF scan mode or product scan mode, using the following operation parameters: GS1 25; GS2 15; Curtain gas 25; ISVF 5500; CE 30; and the accurate mass spectra were recorded. An automated Calibrant Delivery System (ABSCIEX) using a dual nebulizer APCI source that introduces the flow from APCI Positive Calibration Solution (AB SCIEX, Cat# 4460131) to Triple TOF was used to calibrate TOF MS and product ions.</p><p>(C) Positive ion TOF mass spectrum of synthesized [d9-<em>N,N,N</em>-trimethyl]glycerophosphocholine (d9-GPC).</p><p>(D) Collision induced dissociation (CID) mass spectrum of the parent ion at m/z = 267.1663. class="sectionTitle">Introduction</h2><div class="content"><p class="">Recent studies suggest that gut microbes are participants in atherosclerosis development. Specifically, choline, phosphatidylcholine, and carnitinetrimethylamine (TMA)-containing nutrients abundant in foods such as meat, egg yolks, and high-fat dairy productsserve as dietary precursors for TMA N-oxide (TMAO) generation in mice and humans, a metabolite that accelerates atherosclerosis in animal models (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib14" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koeth et al., 2013</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Intestinal microbiota metabolism of L-carnitine, a nutrient in red meat, promotes atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koeth, R.A., Wang, Z., Levison, B.S., Buffa, J.A., Org, E., Sheehy, B.T., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Wu, Y., Li, L. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nat. Toward that end, recent studies confirm that suppression of the host enzyme, FMO3, through an antisense oligonucleotide-based approach, results in the reduction of both circulating TMAO levels and diet-enhanced atherosclerosis in animal models (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib19" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Miao et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Flavin-containing monooxygenase 3 as a potential player in diabetes-associated atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Miao, J., Ling, A.V., Manthena, P.V., Gearing, M.E., Graham, M.J., Crooke, R.M., Croce, K.J., Esquejo, R.M., Clish, C.B., Vicent, D., Biddinger, S.B.,  and Morbid Obesity Study Group. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nat. Commun.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">6</span>: <span class="refPages">6498</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_19_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fncomms7498&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_19_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25849138&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_19_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84927126803&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (6)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib25" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Shih et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Flavin containing monooxygenase 3 exerts broad effects on glucose and lipid metabolism and atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Shih, D.M., Wang, Z., Lee, R., Meng, Y., Che, N., Charugundla, S., Qi, H., Wu, J., Pan, C., Brown, J.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">J. Lipid Res.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">56</span>: <span class="refPages">22&ndash;37</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_25_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1194%2Fjlr.M051680&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_25_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25378658&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_25_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84920704729&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (10)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). However, while numerous beneficial effects with FMO3 inhibition have been noted, FMO3 inhibition is also accompanied by several untoward side effects, including hepatic inflammation (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib25" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Shih et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Flavin containing monooxygenase 3 exerts broad effects on glucose and lipid metabolism and atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Shih, D.M., Wang, Z., Lee, R., Meng, Y., Che, N., Charugundla, S., Qi, H., Wu, J., Pan, C., Brown, J.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">J. Lipid Res.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">56</span>: <span class="refPages">22&ndash;37</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_25_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1194%2Fjlr.M051680&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_25_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25378658&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_25_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84920704729&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (10)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib34" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Warrier et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">The TMAO-Generating Enzyme Flavin Monooxygenase 3 Is a Central Regulator of Cholesterol Balance. </span><span class="refAuthors">Warrier, M., Shih, D.M., Burrows, A.C., Ferguson, D., Gromovsky, A.D., Brown, A.L., Marshall, S., McDaniel, A., Schugar, R.C., Wang, Z. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Cell Rep.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">10</span>: <span class="refPages">326&ndash;338</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_34_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.celrep.2014.12.036&amp;cf=abstract&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Abstract</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_34_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.celrep.2014.12.036&amp;cf=fulltext&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_34_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.celrep.2014.12.036&amp;cf=pdf&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text PDF</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_34_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84921362010&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (10)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). It is also well established that humans with genetic defects in FMO3 suffer from fish odor syndrome, due to the accumulation of TMA and its noxious odor (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib7" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Cashman et al., 2003</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Biochemical and clinical aspects of the human flavin-containing monooxygenase form 3 (FMO3) related to trimethylaminuria. </span><span class="refAuthors">Cashman, J.R., Camp, K., Fakharzadeh, S.S., Fennessey, P.V., Hines, R.N., Mamer, O.A., Mitchell, S.C., Nguyen, G.P., Schlenk, D., Smith, R.L. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Curr. Drug Metab.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2003</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">4</span>: <span class="refPages">151&ndash;170</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_7_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.2174%2F1389200033489505&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_7_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=12678693&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_7_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-0037390952&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (51)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib7">Cashman et al., 2003</span></span>), reducing the appeal of FMO3 as a potential therapeutic target for inhibition. Reduction in TMAO levels by non-lethally targeting the gut microbial pathway(s) involved in TMA production has not yet been explored as a potential therapeutic approach to inhibit atherosclerosis.</p></div></section><section id="sec2" class=""><select name="heading" class="sectionHeading" onChange="window.location.hash=this.value; return false;"><option value="" Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">Identification of an Inhibitor of Microbial TMA Formation from Choline</h3><div class="content"><p class="">Anaerobic choline degradation is believed to serve as the major source of TMA formation within the intestines (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib2" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Bain et al., 2005</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Trimethylamine: metabolic, pharmacokinetic and safety aspects. </span><span class="refAuthors">Bain, M.A., Fornasini, G.,  and Evans, A.M. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Curr. Drug Metab.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2005</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">6</span>: <span class="refPages">227&ndash;240</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_2_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.2174%2F1389200054021807&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_2_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=15975041&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_2_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-19944387962&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (29)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib31" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wang et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Gut flora metabolism of phosphatidylcholine promotes cardiovascular disease. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wang, Z., Klipfell, E., Bennett, B.J., Koeth, R., Levison, B.S., Dugar, B., Feldstein, A.E., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Chung, Y.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nature</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">472</span>: <span class="refPages">57&ndash;63</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnature09922&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=21475195&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-79953733693&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (709)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). Therefore, we focused initial drug screening efforts on the identification of potential inhibitors of microbial TMA production from choline- or so-called choline-TMA lyase activity. The cleavage of the C-N bond of choline to form TMA and acetaldehyde was reported with extracts of <em>Proteus mirabilis</em>, a known human commensal microorganism that is easily cultured, nearly 3 decades ago (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib24" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Sandhu and Chase, 1986</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Aerobic degradation of choline by Proteus mirabilis: enzymatic requirements and pathway. </span><span class="refAuthors">Sandhu, S.S. and Chase, T. Jr. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Can. J. Microbiol.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">1986</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">32</span>: <span class="refPages">743&ndash;750</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_24_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1139%2Fm86-135&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_24_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=3536045&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib24">Sandhu and Chase, 1986</span></span>). Therefore, we initially used <em>P. mirabilis</em> in early drug screening studies. However, during the course of our drug discovery efforts, the identification of a microbial choline TMA lyase was reported as a unique glycyl radical enzyme complex composed of a catalytic polypeptide, CutC, and an associated activating protein, CutD, encoded by adjacent genes within a gene cluster (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib10" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Craciun and Balskus, 2012</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Microbial conversion of choline to trimethylamine requires a glycyl radical enzyme. </span><span class="refAuthors">Craciun, S. and Balskus, E.P. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2012</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">109</span>: <span class="refPages">21307&ndash;21312</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.1215689109&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=23151509&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84871837954&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (67)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib10">Craciun and Balskus, 2012</span></span>). After this report, we subsequently cloned and expressed the <em>cutC/D</em> genes from <em>P. mirabilis</em> and confirmed that the expressed <em>cutC/D</em> gene complex serves as a microbial choline TMA lyase enzyme source (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>A). For example, intact <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells in culture lack choline TMA lyase activity, but following transformation with both <em>P. mirabilis cutC/D</em> genes, they acquire the capacity to enzymatically cleave choline to produce TMA. To further confirm that microbial TMA lyase enzyme activity monitored with this system required the presence of both CutC and CutD, we examined multiple additional controls, including the demonstration of the lack of choline TMA lyase activity following transformation with only <em>cutC</em> or <em>cutD</em> individually or following transformation with <em>cutD</em> but in combination with point mutants of <em>cutC</em> previously reported (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib10" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Craciun and Balskus, 2012</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Microbial conversion of choline to trimethylamine requires a glycyl radical enzyme. </span><span class="refAuthors">Craciun, S. and Balskus, E.P. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2012</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">109</span>: <span class="refPages">21307&ndash;21312</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.1215689109&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=23151509&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84871837954&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (67)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib10">Craciun and Balskus, 2012</span></span>) to lack TMA lyase activity (<em>cutC</em> C781A or G1117A of <em>P. mirabilis</em> were made, which correspond to mutation of the conserved thiyl and glycyl-radical active-site residues C489 and G821 in <em>D. alaskensis</em>) (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>A).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="figs1"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="figs1" title="figs1"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768662/figs1.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure S1. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure S1. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure S1</span></h4><p>Characterization of Choline TMA Lyase Activity of <em>P. mirabilis</em> and Recombinant CutC/D from <em>P. mirabilis</em> and <em>D. alaskensis</em>, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span></p><p>(A) <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells were transformed with empty pBAD vector (negative control), pBAD-<em>cutC/D</em> (from <em>P. mirabilis</em>), or the indicated catalytically inactive <em>cutC</em> point mutants, as described in <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. Cells were grown, CutC/D expression was induced with 0.2% arabinose, and then cells collected by centrifugation, re-suspended in 2x culture volumes of PBS and incubated at 37C in gas tight reaction vials with 100 M of d9-choline substrate. At the indicated time points, TMA lyase activity was measured as d9-TMA production using stable isotope dilution LC/MS/MS.</p><p>(B) Effect of the indicated choline analogs on choline TMA lyase activity of <em>P. mirabilis</em> lysate. Protein lysate (3 mg) was incubated with d9-choline (100 M) in 2 ml PBS with the indicated concentrations of choline analogs (P-choline, betaine, TMSi-ETOH, and DMB) at 37C for 23 hr in gas tight reaction vials and choline TMA lyase activity measured as the production of d9-TMA, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>.</p><p>(C) <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells were transformed with pUC57 plasmid (negative control) or pUC57 expression vectors constitutively expressing <em>D. alaskensis cutC</em> alone, <em>cutD</em> alone, <em>cutC-</em> intergenic region-<em>cutD</em>, or the indicated catalytically inactive <em>cutC</em> point mutants, as described in <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. Bacterial cells at stationary phase (OD<sub>600 nm</sub> = 1.5), were pelleted, re-suspended in 6x original culture volume of cold argon-sparged PBS containing d9-choline (100 M) substrate  DMB (2 mM) and incubated in gas-tight reaction vials under argon blanket at 37C for 12 hr. Choline TMA lyase activity was quantified by the production of d9-TMA by stable isotope dilution LC/MS/MS.</p><p>Data represent mean  SE from three independent replicates (A-C). NA, no addition.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768688/figs1_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=figs1.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=figs1.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><p class="">We surveyed multiple different natural and synthetic structural analogs of choline for their effects on the two microbial choline TMA-lyase activity systems (microbial TMA production using <em>P. mirabilis</em> lysate or recombinant choline TMA lyase activity using <em>cutC/D</em> from <em>P. mirabilis</em> transformed into <em>E. coli</em>) and noted three distinct patterns. Similar results were observed with both systems, with data shown in <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>A being from <em>cutC/D</em>-transformed <em>E. coli</em> cell lysates and illustrative data from <em>P. mirabilis</em> lysates shown in <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>B. The majority of choline analogs showed no effect on TMA lyase activity, which was monitored by quantifying the impact of the analogs on d9-TMA formation from a fixed amount of [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]choline (d9-choline) substrate. One notable example in this category is betaine (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>A; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>B), a choline oxidation metabolite and food component that, we recently showed, can serve as a substrate for gut-microbe-dependent TMA and TMAO generation in vivo, albeit at a rate approximately 100-fold less than that observed with a comparable amount of ingested choline (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib33" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wang et al., 2014b</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Prognostic value of choline and betaine depends on intestinal microbiota-generated metabolite trimethylamine-N-oxide. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wang, Z., Tang, W.H., Buffa, J.A., Fu, X., Britt, E.B., Koeth, R.A., Levison, B.S., Fan, Y., Wu, Y.,  and Hazen, S.L. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Eur. Heart J.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">35</span>: <span class="refPages">904&ndash;910</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_33_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1093%2Feurheartj%2Fehu002&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_33_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=24497336&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_33_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84898859680&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (45)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib33">Wang et al., 2014b</span></span>). Two of the choline analogs examined, 3,3-dimethyl-1-butanol (DMB) and 2-(trimethylsilyl)ethanol (TMSi-ETOH), in which the nitrogen in choline is replaced with either a carbon or silicon atom, respectively, showed roughly comparable <em>cutC/D</em> choline TMA lyase inhibitory activity with a half-maximal inhibitory concentration (IC<sub>50</sub>) in the ~10 M range in the recombinant <em>cutC/D</em> system (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>A). Interestingly, the phosphonium analog of choline (P-choline) showed a unique effect by augmenting <em>cutC/D</em> choline TMA lyase activity (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>A; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>B), suggesting that this choline analog acts as an agonist and may promote more effective interaction between the catalytic (CutC) and activating (CutD) polypeptides. Of the choline TMA lyase activity inhibitors identified, we suspected that DMB, given its structure, might be relatively non-toxic, and possibly even found as a natural product in existing foods or alcoholic beverages. Indeed, screening of multiple fermented liquids, oils, and distilled products confirmed that it is present in some alcoholic beverages and oils to varying levels (e.g., DMB was detected in some balsamic vinegars, in red wines, and in some cold-pressed extra virgin olive oils and grapeseed oils; highest levels observed were 25 mM; data not shown). Therefore, we elected to take DMB forward as a potential tool drug with which to test the hypothesis that inhibiting microbial production of TMA might serve as a potential therapeutic approach for the treatment of atherosclerosis.</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="gr1"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="fig1" title="gr1"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768654/gr1.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure 1. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure 1. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure 1</span></h4><p>The Choline Analog DMB Inhibits Microbial Choline TMA Lyase Activity</p><p>(A) Effect of the indicated choline analogs on microbial TMA lyase activity (measured as d9-TMA production from 100 M of the indicated d9-labeled substrate) from the lysate of <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells transformed (pBAD vector) with <em>cutC/D</em> genes (from <em>P. mirabilis</em>).</p><p>(B) Effect of DMB on choline TMA lyase activity in intact <em>P. mirabilis</em> incubated with the indicated concentrations of d9-choline substrate with or without DMB (1 mM) at 37C. NA, no addition.</p><p>(C) DMB effect on choline uptake. <em>P. mirabilis</em> (OD<sub>600 nm</sub> = 0.5) cells were pelleted and then re-suspended in minimal media supplemented with the indicated concentrations of d9-choline with or without DMB (2 mM) for 15 min at 37C. Intracellular d9-choline was then quantified as described in the <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>.</p><p>(D) Choline TMA lyase activity from intact <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells transformed with the indicated constructs in the presence versus absence of DMB.</p><p>(E) DMB dose-response curves for inhibition of choline TMA lyase activity in intact <em>E. coli</em> Top10 cells transformed with <em>cutC/D</em> genes from either <em>D. alaskensis</em> (pUC57 vector) or <em>P. mirabilis</em> (pBAD vector).</p><p>(F) TMA lyase activity for the indicated substrates in <em>P. mirabilis</em> lysate with or without DMB.</p><p>Data are presented as mean  SE from three independent replicates.</p><p>See also <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figures S1</span></span>, <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">S2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs2">S2</span></span>, and <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs6" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768668/figs6.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs6" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">S6</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs6">S6</span></span>.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768680/gr1_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=gr1.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=gr1.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><p class="">In further in vitro studies, DMB was shown to block TMA production from live microbes, inhibiting the rate of intact <em>P. mirabilis</em> conversion of d9-choline into d9-TMA (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>B). Moreover, parallel studies showed no effect of DMB on choline uptake by the live microbes, indicating that DMB does not block choline uptake into the cells (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>C). Interestingly, while DMB inhibited the choline TMA lyase activity of <em>E. coli</em> transformed with <em>cutC/D</em> from <em>P. mirabilis</em> (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>D), the sensitivity of CutC/D from different microbial species to DMB inhibition varied. For example, transforming <em>E. coli</em> with <em>cutC/D</em> from <em>D. alaskenesis</em>whose homologous CutC and CutD amino acid sequences show 63% and 47% identity to CutC/D from <em>P. mirabilis</em>, respectivelyshowed decreased extent of inhibition with DMB (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>E; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>C). An obligatory role of CutC in the enzymatic cleavage of choline to produce TMA (and DMB inhibition) in <em>E. coli</em> transformed with the <em>cutC/D</em> gene complex from either <em>P. mirabilis</em> or <em>D. alaskensis</em> was supported in both cases by control studies in which the respective <em>cutC</em> gene was mutated to replace previously reported (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib10" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Craciun and Balskus, 2012</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Microbial conversion of choline to trimethylamine requires a glycyl radical enzyme. </span><span class="refAuthors">Craciun, S. and Balskus, E.P. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2012</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">109</span>: <span class="refPages">21307&ndash;21312</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.1215689109&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=23151509&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_10_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84871837954&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (67)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib10">Craciun and Balskus, 2012</span></span>) critical active-site residues involved in catalysis (either C781A or G1117A for CutC from <em>P. mirabilis</em> (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>D) or G489A for CutC from <em>D. alaskensis</em>) (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768675/figs1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs1">Figure S1</span></span>C). Examination of alternative TMA substrates with the recombinant <em>cutC/D</em> system (<em>E. coli</em> transformed with <em>P. mirabilis cutC/D</em>) revealed DMB inhibited conversion of both d9-choline and [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]glycerophosphocholine (d9-GPC) substrates into d9-TMA (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs2">Figures S2</span></span>AS2E). Moreover, when affinity epitope-tagged CutC and CutD (from <em>E. coli</em> Stellar cells transformed with <em>P. mirabilis</em> cutC/D) were individually isolated to homogeneity, addition of DMB to anaerobic reaction mixtures containing both purified polypeptides showed dose-dependent inhibition in TMA production from either choline or GPC substrates (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs2">Figures S2</span></span>FS2H). Further studies showed choline and GPC also serve as preferred substrates over [d9<em>-N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]phosphatidylcholine (d9-PC) and [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]carnitine (d9-carnitine) for TMA production from <em>P. mirabilis</em> lysates, and in both instances, addition of DMB inhibited TMA formation (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span>F).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="figs2"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="figs2" title="figs2"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768659/figs2.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure S2. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure S2. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure S2</span></h4><p>Expression of <em>P. mirabilis</em> CutC/D in <em>E. coli</em> Stellar Cells and the Effects of DMB on CutC/D TMA Lyase Activity, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figure 1</span></span></p><p>(A) <em>E. coli</em> Stellar cells were transformed with <em>pGK::nucMCS</em> plasmid harboring constructs with <em>P. mirabilis cutC/D</em> genes under the control of the <em>Staphylococcus</em> nuclease promoter, <em>Pnuc</em>. CutC was N-terminally tagged with an 8x-His-tag, and CutD was C-terminally tagged with Strep-Tag II, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. As an additional negative control, cells were also transformed with the empty vector. Shown is the SDS-PAGE with Coomassie blue staining of bacterial lysates from the indicated transformed cells. Identity of bands as CutC and CutD was confirmed by excising of each band, and performing LC/MS/MS analysis on in-gel tryptic digests (data not shown).</p><p>(BE) <em>P. mirabilis cutC/D-</em>transformed, or empty-vector transformed <em>E. coli</em> Stellar cells were grown up to stationary phase (OD<sub>600</sub> = 1.5), harvested by centrifugation and re-suspended in 1x PBS with a volume of 6:1 (original culture volume), and then incubated with 100 M of the indicated d9- labeled substrates  DMB (2 mM) at 37C for 24 hr in gas-tight reaction vials, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. As an additional (negative) control, isotope-labeled substrates in PBS without addition of cells were incubated in sealed reaction vials. d9-TMA produced was measured by LC/MS/MS.</p><p>(F) SDS-PAGE analysis of purified CutC after Ni-NTA IMAC affinity chromatography, and purified CutD by Strep-Tactin affinity chromatography.</p><p>(G and H) Purified CutC (30 g) and CutD (30 g) were incubated together in PBS (1 ml) supplemented with Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (2 mM) and S-adenosyl-L-methionine (1 mM) in the presence of isotope-labeled substrate (100 M of either d9-choline (G) or d9-GPC (H)) and the indicated concentrations of DMB, in gas-tight reaction vials under anaerobic conditions at 37C for 12 hr. d9-TMA produced was then quantified by LC/MS/MS. Reactions without CutC or CutD were used as blank controls for each individual d9-TMA containing compound (d9-choline or d9-GPC) and showed negligible (~0.25 nM) d9-TMA production.</p><p>Data (B-E,G,H) represent mean  SE from 3 independent replicates.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" data-unica="fig=figs2.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div></div></section><section id="sec2.2" class=""><select name="heading" class="sectionHeading" Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">DMB Inhibits Some, but Not All, Microbial TMA Lyases</h3><div class="content"><p class="">Since the CutC/D microbial TMA lyase complex does not effectively use either d9-carnitine or d9-PC as substrates (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768672/figs2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs2">Figures S2</span></span>C and S2E), yet both TMA-containing nutrients can be converted into TMA by microbes, we sought to explore whether DMB might serve as an inhibitor to alternative microbial TMA lyase activities. Toward this end, we first cloned the <em>cntA</em> (oxygenase) and <em>cntB</em> (reductase) genes from <em>Acinetobacter baumannii</em>, a recently reported microbial (carnitine-specific) TMA lyase enzyme complex that belongs to the Rieske-type iron-sulfur protein family (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib36" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Zhu et al., 2014</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Carnitine metabolism to trimethylamine by an unusual Rieske-type oxygenase from human microbiota. </span><span class="refAuthors">Zhu, Y., Jameson, E., Crosatti, M., Schfer, H., Rajakumar, K., Bugg, T.D.,  and Chen, Y. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">111</span>: <span class="refPages">4268&ndash;4273</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_36_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.1316569111&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_36_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=24591617&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_36_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84896537331&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (20)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib36">Zhu et al., 2014</span></span>). Examination of the wild-type <em>E. coli</em> BL21 strain showed no carnitine TMA lyase activity, so we transformed these cells with either <em>cntA</em> or <em>cntB</em> (from <em>A. baumannii</em>). While neither transformed <em>E. coli</em> possessed carnitine TMA lyase activity individually, combining the lysates from both <em>cntA</em>- and <em>cntB</em>-transformed <em>E. coli</em> showed the expected acquired enzymatic activity of cleaving d9-carnitine to form d9-TMA (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span>A; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs3">Figure S3</span></span>A). Further confirmation that carnitine TMA lyase activity required the CntA polypeptide was achieved by showing that the lysate from <em>E. coli</em> transformed with a <em>cntA</em> point mutant that was previously shown to lack carnitine TMA lyase activityE205D (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib36" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Zhu et al., 2014</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Carnitine metabolism to trimethylamine by an unusual Rieske-type oxygenase from human microbiota. </span><span class="refAuthors">Zhu, Y., Jameson, E., Crosatti, M., Schfer, H., Rajakumar, K., Bugg, T.D.,  and Chen, Y. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">111</span>: <span class="refPages">4268&ndash;4273</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_36_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.1316569111&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_36_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=24591617&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_36_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84896537331&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (20)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib36">Zhu et al., 2014</span></span>)failed to show activity when combined with <em>cntB</em>-transformed <em>E. coli</em> lysates and incubated with d9-carnitine (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs3">Figure S3</span></span>A). DMB showed no inhibitory effect on CntA/B-catalyzed cleavage of d9-carnitine, and examination of the substrate preference of CntA/B revealed no evidence of TMA production in the presence of alternative potential precursor substrates such as choline, PC, or GPC (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span>A).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="gr2"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="fig2" title="gr2"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768653/gr2.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure 2. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure 2. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure 2</span></h4><p>Inhibitory Effect of DMB on Alternative Microbial TMA Lyases and Both Mouse Cecal and Human Fecal Microbial TMA Lyase Activities with Multiple Substrates</p><p>(A) TMA lyase activity for the indicated substrates (375 M each) in combined lysates from <em>E. coli</em> BL21 cells transformed (pET22 vector) with <em>cntA</em> and <em>cntB</em> genes (from <em>A. baumannii</em>) incubated in the presence or absence of DMB.</p><p>(B) TMA lyase activity with the indicated substrates (375 M, with or without DMB) in intact <em>E. coli</em> BL21 cells transformed (pET22 vector) with <em>yeaW/X</em> genes (from <em>E. coli</em> DH10B).</p><p>(CE) TMA lyase activity with the indicated substrates (with or without DMB) incubated with (C) mouse cecum lysate (1.3 mg/ml protein) or (D and E) human fecal microbes (equivalent to 100 mg/ml). The DMB concentration in all reaction systems was 10 mM (in A and B) or 2 mM (in CE).</p><p>Data are presented as mean  SE from three independent replicates.</p><p>See also <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs3">Figures S3</span></span>, <span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">S6</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">S6</span></span>C, and S6D.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768679/gr2_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=gr2.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=gr2.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="figs3"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="figs3" title="figs3"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768656/figs3.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure S3. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure S3. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure S3</span></h4><p>Characterization of the Effect of DMB on TMA Lyase Activity of the Reiske-type Proteins CntA/B from <em>A. baumannii</em> and YeaW/X from <em>E. coli</em>, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span></p><p>(A) <em>E. coli</em> BL21(DE3) cells were transformed with pET plasmid harboring constructs with <em>A. baumannii</em> (ATCC 19606) synthesized genes (GenScript) for <em>cntA</em> alone, <em>cntB</em> alone, or the catalytically inactive E205D <em>cntA</em> point mutant, as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. As an additional negative control, <em>E. coli</em> were also transformed with empty vector. The indicated bacterial lysates (3 mg protein each) were then assayed for carnitine TMA lyase activity using d9-carnitine (100 M) as substrate in the presence versus absence of DMB (10 mM). Reactions were performed in PBS in the presence of NADPH (200 M) and ammonium iron(II) sulfate hexahydrate (1 mM) at 37C for 16 hr. d9-TMA produced was quantified as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>.</p><p>(B) <em>E. coli</em> BL21 (DE3) cells were transformed with pET plasmid harboring constructs with <em>E. coli</em> DH10B genes for <em>yeaW</em> alone, <em>yeaX</em> alone, <em>yeaW</em> + <em>yeaX</em>, or the catalytically inactive E208D <em>yeaW</em> point mutant + <em>yeaX</em>, as described under Methods. As an additional negative control, <em>E. coli</em> were also transformed with empty pET plasmid. Bacterial cells at stationary phase (OD<sub>600 nm</sub> = 1.5) were harvested by centrifugation and re-suspended in PBS containing d9-choline (100 M) substrate in the presence versus absence of DMB (10 mM) and incubated in gas-tight reaction vials at 37C for 16 hr. Choline TMA lyase activity was quantified by the production of d9-TMA by stable isotope dilution LC/MS/MS as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>.</p><p>(C) SDS-PAGE analysis of purified YeaW and YeaX after Ni-NTA IMAC chromatography.</p><p>(D) Purified YeaW + YeaX (30 g each) were incubated in PBS supplemented with NADH (250 M) and the indicated d9-TMA containing substrates (200 M) in gas-tight vials at 37C for 8 hr. d9-TMA produced was then quantified as described under <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>.</p><p>Data represent mean  SE from 3 independent replicates (A, B, D).</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768682/figs3_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=figs3.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=figs3.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><p class="">We recently reported the cloning and characterization of an alternative TMA lyase enzyme complex from <em>E. coli</em> DH10B strain, YeaW/X, which also appears to be a two-component Rieske-type oxygenase/reductase that, at the amino acid level, shows 71% and 50% sequence identity to CntA and CntB, respectively (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib15" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koeth et al., 2014</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">?-Butyrobetaine is a proatherogenic intermediate in gut microbial metabolism of L-carnitine to TMAO. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koeth, R.A., Levison, B.S., Culley, M.K., Buffa, J.A., Wang, Z., Gregory, J.C., Org, E., Wu, Y., Li, L., Smith, J.D. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Cell Metab.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">20</span>: <span class="refPages">799&ndash;812</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2014.10.006&amp;cf=abstract&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Abstract</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2014.10.006&amp;cf=fulltext&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2014.10.006&amp;cf=pdf&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text PDF</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25440057&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84910129274&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (20)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib15">Koeth et al., 2014</span></span>). Despite the homology to CntA/B, YeaW/X was found to possess broader substrate usage and could produce TMA from either choline or carnitine as substrate (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib15" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koeth et al., 2014</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">?-Butyrobetaine is a proatherogenic intermediate in gut microbial metabolism of L-carnitine to TMAO. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koeth, R.A., Levison, B.S., Culley, M.K., Buffa, J.A., Wang, Z., Gregory, J.C., Org, E., Wu, Y., Li, L., Smith, J.D. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Cell Metab.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">20</span>: <span class="refPages">799&ndash;812</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2014.10.006&amp;cf=abstract&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Abstract</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2014.10.006&amp;cf=fulltext&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2014.10.006&amp;cf=pdf&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text PDF</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25440057&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_15_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84910129274&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (20)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib15">Koeth et al., 2014</span></span>). To examine the impact of DMB on YeaW/X TMA lyase activity, we first transformed <em>E. coli</em> BL21 cells, which show no detectable TMA lyase activity, with either or both of the <em>yeaW/X</em> genes. We observed that DMB inhibits choline TMA lyase enzyme activity in the presence of both YeaW and YeaX (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span>B; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs3">Figure S3</span></span>B). Purified YeaW/X showed enzymatic cleavage of d9-carnitine, [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]?-butyrobetaine (d9-?-butyrobetaine) and [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]crotonobetaine (d9-crotonobetaine), producing d9-TMA. DMB inhibited the conversion of carnitine and crotonobetaine, but not ?-butyrobetaine, into TMA (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768669/figs3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs3">Figures S3</span></span>C and S3D).</p></div></section><section id="sec2.3" class=""><select name="heading" class="sectionHeading" onChange="window.location.hash=this.value; return false;"><option value="" selected="selected">Jump to Section</option><option value="#sec1" class="level_1">Introduction</option><option value="#sec2" class="level_1">Results</option><option Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">DMB Inhibits TMA Formation from Multiple Substrates in Physiological Polymicrobial Cultures</h3><div class="content"><p class="">Next, we examined mouse cecal contents as a physiological polymicrobial source of TMA lyase activities with which the potential inhibitory effect of DMB could be further characterized. Mouse cecal contents were homogenized and lysed under anaerobic conditions and then were incubated with a fixed amount of individual potential d9-labeled TMA precursors (d9-choline, d9-PC, d9-GPC, or d9-carnitine) in the absence versus presence of DMB. All TMA-containing nutrients that we examined served as substrates for TMA production by the polymicrobial enzyme source. Moreover, the addition of DMB inhibited TMA formation from each substrate, suggesting broad inhibitory activity across multiple microbial TMA lyase enzyme activities (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span>C). In a further set of studies, we used human feces from healthy donors as another physiological polymicrobial source of TMA lyase activity. Intact fecal bacteria were recovered and then incubated with a fixed amount of individual d9-labeled potential nutrient precursors (d9-choline, d9-GPC, d9-PC, [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]phosphocholine (d9-phosphocholine), [d9-<em>N,N,N-trimethyl</em>]betaine (d9-betaine), d9-carnitine, d9-?-butyrobetaine, and d9-crotonobetaine) in the absence versus presence of DMB. Both the substrate preference and the inhibitory effect of DMB on human fecal microbial TMA production were assessed. First, all nutrient precursors examined produced TMA, with greater extent of production from nutrients structurally related to choline (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span>D) compared to carnitine (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figure 2</span></span>E). Moreover, DMB significantly inhibited TMA production from each substrate except for betaine and GPC, both of which showed a trend toward inhibition that was not statistically significant (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">Figures 2</span></span>D and 2E).</p></div></section><section id="sec2.4" class=""><select name="heading" class="sectionHeading" onChange="window.location.hash=this.value; return false;"><option value="" selected="selected">Jump to Section</option><option value="#sec1" class="level_1">Introduction</option><option value="#sec2" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Chemical Syntheses</option><option value="#sec4.4" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Mass Spectrometry Quantification of Analytes</option><option value="#sec4.5"
