yeaW/yeaX</option><option value="#sec4.7" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Growth and Induction Conditions for Recombinant TMA Lyases</option><option value="#sec4.8" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Mouse Models</option><option value="#sec4.9" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Bacterial, Mouse Cecum, and Human Feces TMA Lyase Sample Preparation</option><option value="#sec4.10" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Microbiota Studies</option><option value="#sec4.11" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Statistical Analyses</option><option value="#sec5" class="level_1">Author Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">DMB Is a Non-lethal Inhibitor of TMA Production by Microbes in a Rich Nutrient Environment</h3><div class="content"><p class="">Importantly, DMB was shown to serve as a non-lethal inhibitor (i.e., not an antibiotic) in cultures of <em>P. mirabilis</em> grown in a rich nutrient broth under conditions that showed no adverse impact on cell growth despite marked inhibition in microbial TMA lyase activity (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig3">Figure 3</span></span>A). Recently, several human bacterial isolates were identified that are capable of producing TMA from choline in culture, as well as in vivo, when used to colonize germ-free mice (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib23" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Romano et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Intestinal microbiota composition modulates choline bioavailability from diet and accumulation of the proatherogenic metabolite trimethylamine-N-oxide. </span><span class="refAuthors">Romano, K.A., Vivas, E.I., Amador-Noguez, D.,  and Rey, F.E. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">MBio</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">6</span>: <span class="refPages">e02481</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_23_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1128%2FmBio.02481-14&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_23_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84930004775&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (8)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib23">Romano et al., 2015</span></span>). Therefore, we explored the impact of DMB on TMA production and bacterial growth using some of these additional human commensals. Both <em>Proteus penneri</em> and <em>Escherichia fergusonii</em> were confirmed to possess choline TMA lyase activity, readily consuming choline and producing TMA. Similar to results observed with <em>P. mirabilis</em>, addition of DMB to a rich nutrient broth in cultures (as exists in the gut lumen) of either <em>Proteus penneri</em> or <em>Escherichia fergusonii</em> markedly inhibited choline utilization to produce TMA but showed no inhibitory effect on bacterial growth (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig3">Figures 3</span></span>B and 3C).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="gr3"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="fig3" title="gr3"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768652/gr3.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure 3. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure 3. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure 3</span></h4><p>DMB Serves as a Non-lethal Inhibitor</p><p>(AC) <em>P. mirabilis</em> (A), <em>P. penneri</em> (B), and <em>E. fergusonii</em> (C) cells were grown in nutrient broth  DMB (0.1% v/v) and both the growth rate (OD<sub>600</sub>) and total amount of d9-TMA produced from d9-choline (100 M) (insert) were monitored. NA, no addition.</p><p>(D and E) Demonstration that DMB decreases plasma TMAO levels in male (D) and female (E) C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice placed chronically on either a 1.0% choline-supplemented or a 1.0% carnitine-supplemented diet in the presence versus absence of DMB (1%, v/v in drinking water).</p><p>(F) Three groups of male C57BL/6J <em>Apo</em>e<sup>-/-</sup> mice (8 weeks old) were placed on a chemically defined diet equivalent to chow supplemented with choline (1.0% total choline). Two groups of mice were administered 1.2 mol of DMB daily (provided in corn oil; total volume, 150 l) by either (oral) gastric gavage or subcutaneous (SC) route, and the third group of mice received sham (vehicle) gavages daily. After 2 weeks, plasma TMAO levels were determined by LC/MS/MS.</p><p>Data are presented as mean  SE from three independent replicates (AC) or the indicated numbers of independent replicates (DF). class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;General Procedures and Reagents</option><option value="#sec4.2" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Ethical Considerations</option><option value="#sec4.3" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Microbiota Studies</option><option value="#sec4.11" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Statistical Analyses</option><option value="#sec5" class="level_1">Author Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">DMB Lowers Plasma TMAO Levels In Vivo</h3><div class="content"><p class="">To examine the impact of DMB on microbial TMA production in vivo, several studies were performed. First, different groups of C57BL/6J mice were placed on chemically defined diets that were identical in composition except for supplementation with either choline (total choline 1.0%, w/w) or carnitine (1.0%, w/w), diets previously shown to raise TMAO levels and accelerate atherosclerosis in mice (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib14" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koeth et al., 2013</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Intestinal microbiota metabolism of L-carnitine, a nutrient in red meat, promotes atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koeth, R.A., Wang, Z., Levison, B.S., Buffa, J.A., Org, E., Sheehy, B.T., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Wu, Y., Li, L. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nat. Med.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2013</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">19</span>: <span class="refPages">576&ndash;585</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_14_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnm.3145&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_14_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=23563705&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_14_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84877331372&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (358)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib31" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wang et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Gut flora metabolism of phosphatidylcholine promotes cardiovascular disease. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wang, Z., Klipfell, E., Bennett, B.J., Koeth, R., Levison, B.S., Dugar, B., Feldstein, A.E., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Chung, Y.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nature</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">472</span>: <span class="refPages">57&ndash;63</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnature09922&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=21475195&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-79953733693&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (709)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). In addition, at the start of the study, each dietary group of mice was further divided by exposing half to DMB, provided in the drinking water. Provision of DMB resulted in significant reduction in plasma TMAO levels in both the choline-supplemented and the carnitine-supplemented groups, with similar results observed for both male and female mice (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig3">Figures 3</span></span>D and 3E). In further studies, comparison of the effect of route of administration revealed that while orally administered DMB resulted in significant reduction in plasma TMAO levels, a comparable amount of DMB administered subcutaneously failed to reduce plasma TMAO levels (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig3">Figure 3</span></span>F). Next, atherosclerosis-prone apolipoprotein E null mice (<em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup>) at the time of weaning were placed chronically (16 weeks) on the same chemically defined diets comparable to chow (0.07% total choline) versus the same diet supplemented with choline (1.0% total choline) in the presence versus absence of DMB provided in the drinking water. Examination of plasma TMAO levels among the groups of mice showed that while TMAO was markedly elevated in the mice on the choline-supplemented diet (11.8-fold, p &lt; 0.001), addition of DMB again substantially inhibited the choline-diet-induced increase in plasma TMAO concentrations (means  SD, in micromolar, for the four groups: chow, choline, chow + DMB, and choline + DMB, are 1.02  0.09, 22.1  2.1, 0.69  0.06, and 15.9  0.9, respectively; p &lt; 0.01 for comparison of choline versus choline + DMB). Moreover, mice showed no evidence of toxicity to the chronic (16-week) DMB exposure, with no significant increases in circulating lipid levels, plasma choline, glucose or insulin levels, and normal indices of both renal function (creatinine) and liver function (transaminase activities, bilirubin) (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S1</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S1</span></span>). Further tissue biochemical studies confirmed that chronic exposure to DMB did not adversely impact liver lipid (cholesterol, cholesteryl ester, and triglyceride) levels, and histology (including Oil-Red-O and H&amp;E) staining showed no signs of steatohepatitis (data not shown).</p></div></section><section id="sec2.6" class=""><select name="heading" class="sectionHeading" onChange="window.location.hash=this.value; return false;"><option value="" selected="selected">Jump to Section</option><option value="#sec1" class="level_1">Introduction</option><option value="#sec2" class="level_1">Results</option><option value="#sec2.1" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Identification of an Inhibitor of Microbial TMA Formation from Choline</option><option value="#sec2.2" Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">DMB Attenuates Choline-Diet-Enhanced Macrophage Foam Cell Formation and Atherosclerosis</h3><div class="content"><p class="">High total choline content is a characteristic feature of a Western diet. We previously reported that dietary choline supplementation enhanced macrophage cholesterol accumulation, foam cell formation, and atherosclerosis in <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice via microbial TMA/TMAO formation (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib31" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wang et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Gut flora metabolism of phosphatidylcholine promotes cardiovascular disease. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wang, Z., Klipfell, E., Bennett, B.J., Koeth, R., Levison, B.S., Dugar, B., Feldstein, A.E., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Chung, Y.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nature</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">472</span>: <span class="refPages">57&ndash;63</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnature09922&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=21475195&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-79953733693&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (709)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib31">Wang et al., 2011</span></span>). The effect of chronic DMB exposure (in the drinking water) on endogenous peritoneal macrophage foam cell formation was, therefore, next examined in the <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice maintained chronically (16 weeks from time of weaning) on the chow (0.07% total choline) versus choline-supplemented (1.0% total choline) diet. As was previously observed (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib31" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wang et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Gut flora metabolism of phosphatidylcholine promotes cardiovascular disease. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wang, Z., Klipfell, E., Bennett, B.J., Koeth, R., Levison, B.S., Dugar, B., Feldstein, A.E., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Chung, Y.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nature</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">472</span>: <span class="refPages">57&ndash;63</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnature09922&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=21475195&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-79953733693&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (709)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib31">Wang et al., 2011</span></span>), a choline-enriched diet promoted marked increase in endogenous macrophage foam cell generation (approximately 2-fold on choline-supplemented diet; p = 0.02), yet the addition of DMB to the drinking water significantly reduced the observed diet-enhanced macrophage foam cell formation (p &lt; 0.05 for comparison of choline versus choline + DMB; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">Figures 4</span></span>A and 4B ). To assess the impact of DMB on high-choline-diet-dependent enhancement in atherosclerosis in the <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice, parallel studies in male and female mice were performed in similarly treated <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice maintained chronically (16 weeks) from the time of weaning on the chemically defined chow diet (0.07% total choline) versus the choline-supplemented diet (1.0% total choline), in the presence versus absence of DMB (in drinking water). Comparable results (DMB-mediated attenuation of atherosclerosis) were observed in both male and female mice (p = 0.003 and p = 0.01, respectively), with data from males shown in <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">Figures 4</span></span>C and 4D, and results with females shown in <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs4">Figure S4</span></span>A. Briefly, aortic root lesion plaque quantification revealed that mice on a chronic choline-supplemented diet, as expected, showed significant enhancement in atherosclerotic plaque area (approximately 2-fold increase; p = 0.003 males, <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">Figures 4</span></span>C and 4D; p = 0.03 females; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs4">Figure S4</span></span>A) despite no atherogenic changes in plasma lipids (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S1</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S1</span></span>). Remarkably, the addition of DMB significantly inhibited the choline-diet-dependent enhancement in aortic root lesion development in both males (p = 0.006, <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">Figure 4</span></span>D) and females (p = 0.02; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs4">Figure S4</span></span>A). Since observed reductions in plasma TMAO in DMB-treated <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice could also occur via changes in hepatic FMO activity, we also examined total hepatic FMO enzymatic activity in liver homogenates generated from the different groups of mice. Contrary to what one might expect with the reduced TMAO plasma levels, mice exposed to DMB showed not a reduction but, instead, a modest but statistically significant increase in total FMO hepatic enzyme activity (p &lt; 0.0001; <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs4">Figure S4</span></span>B).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="gr4"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="fig4" title="gr4"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768661/gr4.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure 4. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure 4. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure 4</span></h4><p>DMB Attenuates Choline-Enhanced Foam Cell Formation and Atherosclerosis</p><p>(A and B) Representative Oil-Red-O/hematoxylin staining of peritoneal macrophages (A) and foam cell quantification (B) from 20-week-old male C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup>-/-</sup> mice fed chemically defined chow (0.07% total choline) or 1% choline-supplemented diets from the time of weaning (4 weeks). Scale bars in (A), 50 m. Data are presented as mean  SE.</p><p>(C) Representative Oil-Red-O/hematoxylin staining of aortic root sections from 20-week-old male C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup>-/-</sup> mice fed chemically defined chow (0.07% total choline) or choline-supplemented (1.0% total choline) diets in the presence versus absence of DMB provided in the drinking water, as described in the <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. Scale bars, 200 m.</p><p>(D) Aortic root lesion area was quantified in 20-week-old male C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup>-/-</sup> mice from the indicated diet and DMB treatment groups.</p><p>Data are presented as mean  SE. p values shown were calculated by ANOVA. See also <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs4">Figures S4</span></span> and <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768663/figs5.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs5" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">S5</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs5">S5</span></span>.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768687/gr4_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=gr4.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=gr4.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="figs4"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="figs4" title="figs4"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768660/figs4.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure S4. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure S4. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure S4</span></h4><p>DMB Inhibits Atherosclerosis in Female <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> Mice on a High-Choline Diet, Affects Flavin Monooxygenase Activity, and Is Not Detected in Urine if Orally Administered, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr3" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768665/gr3.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr3" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 3</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig3">Figures 3</span></span> and <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">4</span></span></p><p>(A) C57BL/6J Apoe<sup>-/-</sup> mice at time of weaning (4 weeks) were placed on chemically defined chow diet (0.07% total choline) or chow diet supplemented with choline (1.0% total choline), with or without 1% DMB added to the drinking water. At 20 weeks of age, mice were sacrificed. Hearts/aorta from mice were fixed and processed for aortic lesion area quantification, as described in <a href="#sec4">Experimental Procedures</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="sec4">Experimental Procedures</span></span>. Data shown represent mean  SE from the indicated number of female mice per treatment group (data for male mice is shown in main manuscript).</p><p>(B) Liver tissue homogenate from male mice (1 mg/ml protein) was incubated with d9-TMA (100 M) in PBS supplemented with and without NADPH (100 M) at 37C for 8 hr. Reactions were stopped by adding 0.1 N formic acid, and d9-TMAO formed quantified by LC/MS/MS using d4-choline as internal standard. Total hepatic FMO activity was calculated as the NADPH-dependent d9-TMAO formed. Data represent mean  SE from the indicated number of mice.</p><p>(C) Identification of DMB in urine by GC/MS. GC chromatograms of blank (violet), DMB standard spiked into urine (green), urine recovered 30 min after gastric gavage of mice with 1.9 l (15 mol) DMB in 200 l saline (red), urine recovered 30 min after intraperitoneal injection (ip) of mice with 1.9 l (15 mol) DMB in 200 l saline (14.2 M DMB detected) (blue). The red dotted line indicates the retention time of DMB (3,3-dimethyl-1-butanol). p values shown were calculated by ANOVA.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768686/figs4_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=figs4.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=figs4.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><p class="">Despite extensive efforts, we were unable to detect DMB in the plasma or urine of mice chronically exposed to DMB in the drinking water. Moreover, when equivalent amounts of DMB were provided to mice either via intraperitoneal or oral route, DMB was detected in both plasma and urine 30 min following administration via the intraperitoneal route but not following bolus administration by gastric gavage (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768673/figs4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure S4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs4">Figure S4</span></span>C). This suggested the possibility that very rapid hepatic metabolism of the agent may account for the low systemic circulating levels observed following oral administration, absorption, and portal blood delivery of DMB to the liver. Consistent with this hypothesis, DMB was found to be rapidly metabolized by liver alcohol dehydrogenase at a rate comparable to that for the substrate ethanol (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768663/figs5.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-figs5" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures S5</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="figs5">Figures S5</span></span>A and S5B). We could not detect the initial predicted metabolite, 3,3-dimethylbutyric aldehyde, in plasma or urine, but instead found evidence of further rapid metabolism to the carboxylic acid, with detection of the metabolite, 3,3-dimethylbutyrylcarnitine, in urine (structure confirmed by high-resolution mass spectrometry; data not shown).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="figs5"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="figs5" title="figs5"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768663/figs5.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768650/figs5.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768663/figs5.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure S5. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure S5. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure S5</span></h4><p>Liver Metabolism of DMB, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr4" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768674/gr4.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr4" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 4</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig4">Figure 4</span></span></p><p>The indicated units of liver alcohol dehydrogenase were incubated in sodium phosphate buffer (50 mM, pH 7.0) supplemented with NAD<sup>+</sup> (7.5 mM) and either ethanol or DMB (each 3.5%) at 25C with spectra (280-380 nm) serially acquired over time (Spectra Max Plus, Molecular Devices, Sunnyvale, CA). Representative optical absorption spectra shown are from comparable time points for ethanol (A) and DMB (B) incubations, and represent the formation of the product, NADH, with the oxidation of alcohol.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743figs5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768663/figs5.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768676/figs5_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=figs5.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=figs5.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><p class="">It has previously been reported that 30 min after intraperitoneal administration of DMB, through competitive inhibition of choline dehydrogenase, the choline content of liver tissues increases (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib3" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Barlow and Marchbanks, 1985</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">The effects of inhibiting choline dehydrogenase on choline metabolism in mice. </span><span class="refAuthors">Barlow, P. and Marchbanks, R.M. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Biochem. Pharmacol.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">1985</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">34</span>: <span class="refPages">3117&ndash;3122</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_3_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2F0006-2952%2885%2990156-X&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_3_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=3899120&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_3_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-0022411833&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (3)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib3">Barlow and Marchbanks, 1985</span></span>). Choline dehydrogenase is expressed mainly in the liver and kidney and catalyzes conversion of choline into betaine aldehyde, an intermediate in the generation of betaine (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib6" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Cafiero, 1951</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">[Choline dehydrogenase of the liver]. </span><span class="refAuthors">Cafiero, M. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Boll. Soc. Ital. Biol. Sper.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">1951</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">27</span>: <span class="refPages">1426&ndash;1427</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_6_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=14904746&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib26" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Tan et al., 1981</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Histochemical findings suggesting that methylazoxymethanol, a liver and kidney carcinogen, is a substrate for hepatic and renal choline dehydrogenase. </span><span class="refAuthors">Tan, Q.H., Penkovsky, L.,  and Zedeck, M.S. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Carcinogenesis</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">1981</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">2</span>: <span class="refPages">1135&ndash;1139</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_26_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1093%2Fcarcin%2F2.11.1135&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_26_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=7032741&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). Therefore, in additional studies, we measured levels of choline, betaine, TMA, and TMAO in liver and kidney, as well as plasma and urine, from C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice chronically administered DMB in the drinking water. No evidence of tissue choline elevation was noted in either liver or kidney following chronic oral DMB treatment in mice; however, modest elevations in plasma and kidney betaine levels were observed in mice on choline diet with DMB (versus choline diet without DMB) (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S2</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S2</span></span>). Notably, as expected with provision of an inhibitor of microbial TMA production, the most significant effects detected with DMB exposure were reductions in TMA and TMAO levels in all tissues and fluids examined (liver, kidney, plasma, and urine), especially under conditions of choline diet supplementation (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S2</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S2</span></span>).</p></div></section><section id="sec2.7" Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h3 class="sectionTitle">DMB Reduces the Proportions of Some Taxa Associated with TMAO Levels</h3><div class="content"><p class="">One theoretical advantage of a therapeutic agent that functions as a non-lethal, microbial-enzyme-targeted inhibitor, as opposed to an antibiotic, is that there should be less selective pressure for development of resistance. Nonetheless, the gut microbiome is known to be remarkably dynamic and adaptive, including changes in response to different dietary inputs, as well as the presence versus absence of distinct diseases (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib9" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Cox et al., 2014</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Altering the intestinal microbiota during a critical developmental window has lasting metabolic consequences. </span><span class="refAuthors">Cox, L.M., Yamanishi, S., Sohn, J., Alekseyenko, A.V., Leung, J.M., Cho, I., Kim, S.G., Li, H., Gao, Z., Mahana, D. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Cell</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">158</span>: <span class="refPages">705&ndash;721</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cell.2014.05.052&amp;cf=abstract&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Abstract</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cell.2014.05.052&amp;cf=fulltext&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cell.2014.05.052&amp;cf=pdf&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text PDF</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25126780&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84907563983&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (91)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib16" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koren et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Human oral, gut, and plaque microbiota in patients with atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koren, O., Spor, A., Felin, J., Fk, F., Stombaugh, J., Tremaroli, V., Behre, C.J., Knight, R., Fagerberg, B., Ley, R.E.,  and Bckhed, F. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">108</span>: <span class="refPages">4592&ndash;4598</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_16_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.1011383107&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_16_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=20937873&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_16_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-79952758083&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (191)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib35" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wu et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Linking long-term dietary patterns with gut microbial enterotypes. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wu, G.D., Chen, J., Hoffmann, C., Bittinger, K., Chen, Y.Y., Keilbaugh, S.A., Bewtra, M., Knights, D., Walters, W.A., Knight, R. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Science</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">334</span>: <span class="refPages">105&ndash;108</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_35_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1126%2Fscience.1208344&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_35_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=21885731&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_35_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-80053618114&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (897)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). Therefore, we sought to examine whether chronic exposure to DMB may alter TMAO levels not only via inhibition in microbial TMA production but also by potentially inducing reorganization of the gut microbial community. Cecal microbial compositions were analyzed in both male and female mice in the atherosclerosis studies by sequencing 16S rRNA gene amplicons in the various <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice groups. Global analyses of community structure showed differences between the chow-fed (0.07% choline) versus high-choline-diet-fed (1.0%) groups, which became more pronounced in the presence of DMB (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768664/gr5.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr5" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 5</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig5">Figures 5</span></span>A and 5C ). Further examination revealed that the proportions of several taxa within all groups of mice within each gender were associated with aortic root lesion area and plasma TMA and TMAO levels to some extent (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Tables S3</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Tables S3</span></span> and <span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">S4</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">S4</span></span>). For example, among male mice, proportions of the taxon <em>Clostridiaceae</em> were highly positively correlated with plasma TMA and TMAO levels, as well as with atherosclerotic lesion area (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S3</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S3</span></span>). Furthermore, male mice placed on a choline-supplemented diet showed a significant increase in the cecal proportion of this taxon, and exposure to DMB promoted a strong tendency (p = 0.06) to decrease the proportion of this taxon in mice fed a choline-supplemented diet (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768664/gr5.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr5" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 5</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig5">Figure 5</span></span>B).</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="gr5"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="fig5" title="gr5"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768664/gr5.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768651/gr5.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768664/gr5.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure 5. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure 5. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure 5</span></h4><p>DMB Alters Gut Microbial Composition</p><p>(AE) Unweighted UniFrac distances plotted in principal-component analysis (PCoA) space comparing cecal microbial communities in male (A) or female (C) C57BL/6J <em>Apoe</em><sup>-/-</sup> mice fed chow versus choline-supplemented diet in the presence versus absence of DMB. (B, D, and E) Impact of diet and DMB on the proportion of several taxa.</p><p>Data are presented as mean  SE (n = 9 mice per group). Similarly, the prevalence of the taxon <em>Lachnospiraceae</em> was highly associated with TMA and TMAO levels and showed a trend toward increased aortic root plaque area. It is also interesting to note that the proportion of the taxon <em>Clostridiales</em>, the most abundant bacterial order observed in cecum, was positively correlated to plasma TMA levels (r = 0.35, p = 0.03), and many <em>Clostridiales</em> species have been reported to cleave choline (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib20" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Mller et al., 1986</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Degradation of various amine compounds by mesophilic clostridia. </span><span class="refAuthors">Mller, B., Hippe, H.,  and Gottschalk, G. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Arch. Microbiol.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">1986</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">145</span>: <span class="refPages">85&ndash;90</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_20_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1007%2FBF00413032&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_20_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=3753143&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_20_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-0022529568&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (24)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib20">Mller et al., 1986</span></span>). Furthermore, female mice placed on a choline-supplemented diet showed a significant increase in the proportions of this taxon, and exposure to DMB induced a significant decrease in the proportions of this taxon (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768664/gr5.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr5" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 5</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig5">Figure 5</span></span>D; <span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S4</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB
    )
                    
                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S4</span></span>). In contrast, proportions of <em>S24-7</em>, an abundant family from <em>Bacterioidetes</em>, showed significant inverse associations with both atherosclerotic plaque area and TMA levels and a trend toward reduced plasma TMAO (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr5" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768664/gr5.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr5" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 5</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig5">Figure 5</span></span>E; <span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S4</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download

                </a></span><br></br><br></br><span class="label">Document S1. Supplemental Experimental Procedures and Tables S1S4</span></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="mmc1">Table S4</span></span>). Indeed, within the female mice, examination of the impact of DMB on multiple taxa associated with TMA or TMAO levels and atherosclerotic plaque extent indicates that provision of the agent induced a reduction in the levels of many bacterial taxa positively associated with levels of TMA, TMAO, or aortic root lesion area and, conversely, a rise in the proportions of many of the bacterial taxa negatively associated with TMA, TMAO, or aortic lesion area (<span class="externalFile"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick fileLink" id="back-mmc1">Table S4</a><span class="externalFileLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="download"><a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768690/mmc1.pdf"><img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_marlin/images/icon_pdf.gif" alt="pdf icon"></img>Download
                    (.35 MB

yeaW/yeaX</option><option value="#sec4.7" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Growth and Induction Conditions for Recombinant TMA Lyases</option><option value="#sec4.8" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Mouse Contributions</option><option value="#app2" class="level_1">Supplemental Information</option><option value="#cebib0010" class="level_1">References</option></select><h2 class="sectionTitle">Discussion</h2><div class="content"><p class="">Recent advances identify a pivotal role for gut microbiota in multiple phenotypes associated with cardiometabolic diseases. A causal role for the involvement of gut microbes is, in part, based on the fulfillment of one of Kochs postulates: namely, the transmission of a disease-related phenotype with the transplantation of gut microbial organisms to a new host (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib8" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Cho and Blaser, 2012</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">The human microbiome: at the interface of health and disease. </span><span class="refAuthors">Cho, I. and Blaser, M.J. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nat. Rev. Genet.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2012</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">13</span>: <span class="refPages">260&ndash;270</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_8_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=22411464&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib9" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Cox et al., 2014</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Altering the intestinal microbiota during a critical developmental window has lasting metabolic consequences. </span><span class="refAuthors">Cox, L.M., Yamanishi, S., Sohn, J., Alekseyenko, A.V., Leung, J.M., Cho, I., Kim, S.G., Li, H., Gao, Z., Mahana, D. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Cell</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2014</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">158</span>: <span class="refPages">705&ndash;721</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cell.2014.05.052&amp;cf=abstract&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Abstract</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cell.2014.05.052&amp;cf=fulltext&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cell.2014.05.052&amp;cf=pdf&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text PDF</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25126780&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_9_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84907563983&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (91)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib11" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Dumas et al., 2006</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Metabolic profiling reveals a contribution of gut microbiota to fatty liver phenotype in insulin-resistant mice. </span><span class="refAuthors">Dumas, M.E., Barton, R.H., Toye, A., Cloarec, O., Blancher, C., Rothwell, A., Fearnside, J., Tatoud, R., Blanc, V., Lindon, J.C. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2006</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">103</span>: <span class="refPages">12511&ndash;12516</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_11_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1073%2Fpnas.0601056103&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_11_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=16895997&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_11_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-33747618036&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (432)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib12" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Gregory et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Transmission of atherosclerosis susceptibility with gut microbial transplantation. </span><span class="refAuthors">Gregory, J.C., Buffa, J.A., Org, E., Wang, Z., Levison, B.S., Zhu, W., Wagner, M.A., Bennett, B.J., Li, L., DiDonato, J.A. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">J. Biol. Chem.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">290</span>: <span class="refPages">5647&ndash;5660</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_12_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1074%2Fjbc.M114.618249&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_12_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25550161&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_12_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84923913674&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (11)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib13" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koch, 1880</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Investigations into the Etiology of Traumatic Infective Diseases. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koch, R. </span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_13_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.5962%2Fbhl.title.106951&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib24" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Sandhu and Chase, 1986</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Aerobic degradation of choline by Proteus mirabilis: enzymatic requirements and pathway. </span><span class="refAuthors">Sandhu, S.S. and Chase, T. Jr. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Can. J. Microbiol.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">1986</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">32</span>: <span class="refPages">743&ndash;750</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_24_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1139%2Fm86-135&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_24_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=3536045&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). The recognition of gut microbes as participants in cardiovascular and metabolic disease pathogenesis suggests that pharmacological interventions aimed at drugging the microbiome may potentially serve as a therapeutic approach for the treatment of cardiometabolic diseases. Toward this end, in the absence of knowledge of specific microbial enzyme activities or their metabolites mechanistically linked to disease phenotypes, therapeutic approaches (aside from dietary restrictions) have, thus far, instead focused on altering gut microbial community composition through the use of either defined microbial compositions (probiotics) or non-microbial substances that may secondarily foster alteration in microbial community structure (prebiotics) (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib21" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Petschow et al., 2013</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Probiotics, prebiotics, and the host microbiome: the science of translation. </span><span class="refAuthors">Petschow, B., Dor, J., Hibberd, P., Dinan, T., Reid, G., Blaser, M., Cani, P.D., Degnan, F.H., Foster, J., Gibson, G. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Ann. N Y Acad. Sci.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2013</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">1306</span>: <span class="refPages">1&ndash;17</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_21_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1111%2Fnyas.12303&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_21_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=24266656&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_21_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84890122259&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (17)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib21">Petschow et al., 2013</span></span>). Multiple lines of evidence show that the meta-organismal pathway involved in TMAO production is mechanistically linked to atherosclerosis susceptibility (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib4" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Bennett et al., 2013</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Trimethylamine-N-oxide, a metabolite associated with atherosclerosis, exhibits complex genetic and dietary regulation. </span><span class="refAuthors">Bennett, B.J., de Aguiar Vallim, T.Q., Wang, Z., Shih, D.M., Meng, Y., Gregory, J., Allayee, H., Lee, R., Graham, M., Crooke, R. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Cell Metab.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2013</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">17</span>: <span class="refPages">49&ndash;60</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_4_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2012.12.011&amp;cf=abstract&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Abstract</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_4_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2012.12.011&amp;cf=fulltext&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_4_2&amp;dbid=4&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.cmet.2012.12.011&amp;cf=pdf&amp;site=cell-site" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Full Text PDF</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_4_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=23312283&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_4_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84872139636&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (83)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib12" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Gregory et al., 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Transmission of atherosclerosis susceptibility with gut microbial transplantation. </span><span class="refAuthors">Gregory, J.C., Buffa, J.A., Org, E., Wang, Z., Levison, B.S., Zhu, W., Wagner, M.A., Bennett, B.J., Li, L., DiDonato, J.A. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">J. Biol. Chem.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">290</span>: <span class="refPages">5647&ndash;5660</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_12_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1074%2Fjbc.M114.618249&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_12_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25550161&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_12_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84923913674&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (11)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib14" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Koeth et al., 2013</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Intestinal microbiota metabolism of L-carnitine, a nutrient in red meat, promotes atherosclerosis. </span><span class="refAuthors">Koeth, R.A., Wang, Z., Levison, B.S., Buffa, J.A., Org, E., Sheehy, B.T., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Wu, Y., Li, L. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nat. Med.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2013</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">19</span>: <span class="refPages">576&ndash;585</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_14_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnm.3145&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_14_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=23563705&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_14_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84877331372&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (358)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>, <span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib31" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Wang et al., 2011</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">Gut flora metabolism of phosphatidylcholine promotes cardiovascular disease. </span><span class="refAuthors">Wang, Z., Klipfell, E., Bennett, B.J., Koeth, R., Levison, B.S., Dugar, B., Feldstein, A.E., Britt, E.B., Fu, X., Chung, Y.M. et al. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Nature</span>. <span class="refSeriesDate">2011</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">472</span>: <span class="refPages">57&ndash;63</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1038%2Fnature09922&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=21475195&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_31_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-79953733693&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (709)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span>). However, the effect of small-molecule antagonists for the critical initiating microbial TMA lyase step on atherosclerosis development has not yet been reported.</p><p class="">The present studies provide proof of concept for the idea that inhibiting microbial TMA production, through microbial TMA lyase inhibition, may serve as a potential therapeutic approach for the prevention or treatment of atherosclerosis (<a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr6" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768670/gr6.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr6" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figure 6</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig6">Figure 6</span></span>). DMB, a structural analog of choline, served as a preliminary tool compound to inhibit microbe-dependent TMA and host TMAO formation from multiple distinct TMA-containing nutrients in vitro and in vivo. Moreover, DMB provision, despite no significant effects on circulating cholesterol, choline, and other pro-atherogenic risk factors, inhibited choline-diet-dependent accumulation of both macrophage cholesteryl ester accumulation (foam cell formation) and aortic root atherosclerotic plaque development. In addition to attenuating plasma levels of TMAO, DMB promoted reduction in proportions of some microbial taxa that are associated with plasma TMA and TMAO levels, as well as aortic plaque extent. While the impact of DMB on microbial composition was noticeable, and while one might anticipate that there is less selective pressure for development of resistance against a non-lethal enzyme inhibitor compared to an antibiotic, the fact that there is any change in microbial composition induced by DMB suggests that some degree of selective pressure is occurring with exposure to the agent and thus raises the possibility for the development of resistance.</p><div class="floatDisplay"><span id="gr6"></span><div class="fig-wrapper" id="fig6" title="gr6"><div><span class="fig-img-wrap"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr6" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768670/gr6.jpg" target="new" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg"><img src="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768657/gr6.sml" data-downsampled="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768670/gr6.jpg" alt="Thumbnail image of Figure 6. Opens large image" title="Thumbnail image of Figure 6. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure 6</span></h4><p>Schema Showing Use of DMB to Inhibit Gut Microbial TMA Production for the Treatment of Atherosclerosis</p><p>DMB is a structural analog of choline and an inhibitor of microbial TMA production (a choline TMA lyase inhibitor). Provision of DMB in the drinking water of atherosclerosis-prone <em>Apoe</em><sup><em>-/-</em></sup> mice inhibits choline-diet-dependent enhancement in TMAO, endogenous macrophage foam cell formation, and atherosclerosis development.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr6" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768670/gr6.jpg" target="_blank" name="modal" class="zoomLink" data-class="largeImg">View Large Image</a> | <a href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768683/gr6_lrg.jpg" target="new">View Hi-Res Image</a> | <a href="/action/downloadFigures?pii=S0092867415015743&amp;id=gr6.jpg" onclick="return doUnica(this);" data-unica="fig=gr6.jpg">Download PowerPoint Slide</a></div></div></div></div><p class="">Gut microbes serve as a filter for our largest environmental exposurewhat we eat. They also function, fundamentally, as a dynamic endocrine organ, generating biologically active metabolites in response to specific nutrient inputs that can enter the circulation and elicit biological effects at distant sites within the host (<span class="bibRef"><a href="javascript:void(0);" id="back-bib5" class="layerTrigger layerTriggerClick">Brown and Hazen, 2015</a><span class="hoverLayer refLayer"><span class="closeBtn" role="button" tabindex="0" aria-label="close window">x</span><span class="refTitle">The gut microbial endocrine organ: bacterially derived signals driving cardiometabolic diseases. </span><span class="refAuthors">Brown, J.M. and Hazen, S.L. </span><span class="refSeriesTitle">Annu. Rev. Med.</span> <span class="refSeriesDate">2015</span>;
        <span class="refSeriesVolume">66</span>: <span class="refPages">343&ndash;359</span><br></br><span class="reference"><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_5_2&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=10.1146%2Fannurev-med-060513-093205&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">CrossRef</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_5_2&amp;dbid=8&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=25587655&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">PubMed</a> | <a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=e_1_5_1_2_5_2&amp;dbid=137438953472&amp;doi=10.1016/j.cell.2015.11.055&amp;key=2-s2.0-84921417572&amp;cf=" onclick="newWindow(this.href);return false">Scopus (10)</a></span><a href="#references" class="allReferencesLink">See all References</a></span></span><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="bib5">Brown and Hazen, 2015</span></span>). The present studies suggest that the development of selective, non-lethal microbial enzyme inhibitors that target causative pathways, once they are revealed, may serve as an effective and complementary strategy to current approaches for the prevention and treatment of cardiometabolic diseases.</p></div></section><section id="sec4" class="materials-methods"><select name="heading" class="sectionHeading" onChange="window.location.hash=this.value; return false;"><option value="" selected="selected">Jump to Section</option><option value="#sec1" class="level_1">Introduction</option><option value="#sec2" class="level_1">Results</option><option value="#sec2.1" class="level_2">&nbsp;&nbsp;Identification of an Inhibitor of Microbial TMA Formation Procedures and Reagents</h3><div class="content"><p class="">Reagents were purchased from Sigma unless otherwise stated. Routine blood tests were measured on a Roche Cobas Analyzer. Insulin was measured by ELISA with the Mouse Insulin ELISA Kit (Mercodia). Protein concentration was determined by bicinchoninic acid (BCA) assay. Opens large image"></img></a></span><div class="caption"><h4><span class="label">Figure S6</span></h4><p>Analysis of Synthesized P-Choline and d9-GPC by High-Resolution Mass Spectrometry, Related to <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr1" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768667/gr1.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr1" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">Figures 1</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig1">Figures 1</span></span> and <a data-link="modal" data-target="#image-S0092867415015743gr2" href="/cms/attachment/2043164566/2055768666/gr2.jpg" target="_blank" id="back-gr2" class="figureLink" data-class="largeImg">2</a><span class="hidden crossref-label"><span class="fig2">2</span></span></p><p>(A) Positive ion TOF mass spectrum of synthesized (2-hydroxyethyl)(trimethyl) phosphonium (P-choline).</p><p>(B) Collision induced dissociation (CID) mass spectrum of the parent ion at m/z = 121.0777. The prepared compound, P-choline, was dissolved in water at a concentration of 50 M, and was injected onto an AB SCIEX TripleTOF 5600 LC-MS/MS System at a flow rate of 10 l/min with an electrospray interface operating in positive TOF scan mode or product scan mode, using the following operation parameters: GS1 25; GS2 15; Curtain gas 25; ISVF 5500; CE 30; and the accurate mass spectra were recorded. An automated Calibrant Delivery System (ABSCIEX) using a dual nebulizer APCI source that introduces the flow from APCI Positive Calibration Solution (AB SCIEX, Cat# 4460131) to Triple TOF was used to calibrate TOF MS and product ions.</p><p>(C) Positive ion TOF mass spectrum of synthesized [d9-<em>N,N,N</em>-trimethyl]glycerophosphocholine (d9-GPC).</p><p>(D) Collision induced dissociation (CID) mass spectrum of the parent ion at m/z = 267.1663. The prepared compound, d9-GPC, was dissolved in water at a concentration of 50 M, and was injected to AB SCIEX TripleTOF 5600 LC-MS/MS System as above.</p><p></p></div><div class="fig-links"><a
